###begin article-title 0
###xml 35 41 35 41 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">foxi3a</italic>
###xml 46 52 46 52 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">foxi3b</italic>
###xml 107 116 <span type="species:ncbi:7955">Zebrafish</span>
A Positive Regulatory Loop between foxi3a and foxi3b Is Essential for Specification and Differentiation of Zebrafish Epidermal Ionocytes
###end article-title 0
###begin p 1
Conceived and designed the experiments: YJ CH PH. Performed the experiments: CH YG. Analyzed the data: YJ CH PH. Contributed reagents/materials/analysis tools: YJ MM MY. Wrote the paper: YJ CH PH.
###end p 1
###begin title 2
Background
###end title 2
###begin p 3
Epidermal ionocytes play essential roles in the transepithelial transportation of ions, water, and acid-base balance in fish embryos before their branchial counterparts are fully functional. However, the mechanism controlling epidermal ionocyte specification and differentiation remains unknown.
###end p 3
###begin title 4
Methodology/Principal Findings
###end title 4
###begin p 5
###xml 826 827 826 827 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 829 830 829 830 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 854 855 854 855 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 3 12 <span type="species:ncbi:7955">zebrafish</span>
In zebrafish, we demonstrated that Delta-Notch-mediated lateral inhibition plays a vital role in singling out epidermal ionocyte progenitors from epidermal stem cells. The entire epidermal ionocyte domain of genetic mutants and morphants, which failed to transmit the DeltaC-Notch1a/Notch3 signal from sending cells (epidermal ionocytes) to receiving cells (epidermal stem cells), differentiates into epidermal ionocytes. The low Notch activity in epidermal ionocyte progenitors is permissive for activating winged helix/forkhead box transcription factors of foxi3a and foxi3b. Through gain- and loss-of-function assays, we show that the foxi3a-foxi3b regulatory loop functions as a master regulator to mediate a dual role of specifying epidermal ionocyte progenitors as well as of subsequently promoting differentiation of Na+,K+-ATPase-rich cells and H+-ATPase-rich cells in a concentration-dependent manner.
###end p 5
###begin title 6
Conclusions/Significance
###end title 6
###begin p 7
This study provides a framework to show the molecular mechanism controlling epidermal ionocyte specification and differentiation in a low vertebrate for the first time. We propose that the positive regulatory loop between foxi3a and foxi3b not only drives early ionocyte differentiation but also prevents the complete blockage of ionocyte differentiation when the master regulator of foxi3 function is unilaterally compromised.
###end p 7
###begin title 8
Introduction
###end title 8
###begin p 9
###xml 442 445 442 445 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0000302-Wall1">[1]</xref>
###xml 447 450 447 450 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0000302-AlAwqati1">[2]</xref>
###xml 608 611 608 611 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0000302-Hulander1">[3]</xref>
###xml 950 953 950 953 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0000302-Perry1">[4]</xref>
###xml 955 958 955 958 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0000302-Evans1">[5]</xref>
###xml 1393 1396 1393 1396 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0000302-Varsamos1">[6]</xref>
###xml 1398 1401 1398 1401 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0000302-Kaneko1">[7]</xref>
###xml 1403 1406 1403 1406 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0000302-Hwang1">[8]</xref>
###xml 1683 1686 1683 1686 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0000302-Chang1">[9]</xref>
###xml 1688 1692 1688 1692 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0000302-Hiroi1">[10]</xref>
###xml 2230 2234 2230 2234 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0000302-Briggs1">[11]</xref>
###xml 2236 2240 2236 2240 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0000302-Ackermann1">[12]</xref>
###xml 2242 2246 2242 2246 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0000302-Chen1">[13]</xref>
###xml 2310 2311 2310 2311 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 2310 2311 2310 2311 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><sup>+</sup></italic>
###xml 2310 2311 2310 2311 <bold xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><italic><sup>+</sup></italic></bold>
###xml 2313 2314 2313 2314 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 2313 2314 2313 2314 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><sup>+</sup></italic>
###xml 2313 2314 2313 2314 <bold xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><italic><sup>+</sup></italic></bold>
###xml 2346 2347 2346 2347 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 2346 2347 2346 2347 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><sup>+</sup></italic>
###xml 2346 2347 2346 2347 <bold xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><italic><sup>+</sup></italic></bold>
###xml 2401 2405 2401 2405 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0000302-Lin1">[14]</xref>
###xml 428 433 <span type="species:ncbi:10090">mouse</span>
###xml 459 464 <span type="species:ncbi:10090">mouse</span>
###xml 2074 2083 <span type="species:ncbi:7955">zebrafish</span>
###xml 2376 2385 <span type="species:ncbi:7955">zebrafish</span>
###xml 2553 2562 <span type="species:ncbi:7955">zebrafish</span>
In terrestrial vertebrates, diverse types of ionocytes (ICs) that are distributed in the bladder, inner ear, and kidney have evolved to play essential roles in transepithelial ion, water, and acid-base transportation. For instance, ICs specializing in mediating salt and water absorption (principal cells), acid-base transport (intercalated cells), or both (inner medullary cells) have been characterized in collecting ducts of mouse kidneys [1], [2]. In the mouse inner ear, forkhead-related (FORE) cells are also reported to play an essential role in maintaining osmotic pressure and the ionic composition [3]. For aquatic vertebrates like fish, the maintenance of osmotic and ionic gradients between body fluids and the external environment for internal homeostasis are more challenging. Fish gills have evolved as the most important extra-renal organ to mediate ion transport and acid-base regulation for compensating the passive leakage of ions [4], [5]. At a cellular level, the functional units of ion and acid-base regulation in gills are carried out by branchial ICs (also known as chloride cells or mitochondrion-rich cells) which are primarily deposited on the junction regions between filaments and lamellae. At an organismic level, when the functional gill is not yet completely developed, epidermal ICs are precociously scattered on the skin to mediate ion and acid-base balance [6], [7], [8]. Based on morphology, vital dye binding and immunocytochemistry, distinct types of epidermal or branchial ICs have been reported in diverse fish species, and have been suggested to mediate an equivalent function as their counterparts in the kidneys of terrestrial vertebrates [9], [10]. However, very little is known about whether distinct types of ICs are generated from common progenitors and what the molecular mechanisms controlling IC specification and differentiation are. We noted that the slow progress of studying IC development in aquatic animals is largely caused by insufficient molecular and genetic tools for non-model fish. Therefore, we sought to use zebrafish as a new model to study IC development by taking advantage of a mature embryonic manipulation technique as well as many available genetic mutants [11], [12], [13]. Recently, our team identified two types of epidermal ICs as Na+,K+-ATPase-rich cells (NaRCs) and H+-ATPase-rich cells (HRCs) in zebrafish embryonic skin [14]. In this study, we extend our knowledge to the molecular mechanisms determining IC identity and promoting the differentiation of NaRCs and HRCs in zebrafish.
###end p 9
###begin p 10
###xml 180 184 180 184 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0000302-Lai1">[15]</xref>
###xml 193 203 193 203 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">Drosophila</italic>
###xml 402 406 402 406 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0000302-Hartenstein1">[16]</xref>
###xml 835 858 831 854 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">Hairy/Enhancer of Split</italic>
###xml 973 977 969 973 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">AS-C</italic>
###xml 1095 1099 1091 1095 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0000302-Ghysen1">[17]</xref>
###xml 1101 1105 1097 1101 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0000302-Heitzler1">[18]</xref>
###xml 1206 1216 1202 1212 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">Drosophila</italic>
###xml 1216 1220 1212 1216 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0000302-Heitzler2">[19]</xref>
###xml 1225 1234 <span type="species:ncbi:7955">zebrafish</span>
###xml 1798 1807 <span type="species:ncbi:7955">zebrafish</span>
The Delta-Notch (D-N) lateral inhibition mechanism is an evolutionarily conserved signal transduction cascade and plays essential roles in cell fate choices and boundary formation [15]. Taking Drosophila sensory organ formation as an example, the position of the sensory organ precursor (SOP) is determined by the basic helix-loop-helix (bHLH) transcription factors of the Achaete-Scute complex (AS-C) [16]. Within the SOP cluster, a nascent SOP expresses cell surface DSL (Delta, Serrate, Lag-2) ligands, and activates Notch in neighboring cells. Activation of the Notch pathway results in proteolytic cleavage to release the intracellular domain of Notch (NICD), which is subsequently translocated into the nucleus and is associated with CSL (CBF1/Su(H)/Lag-1, also known as RBPjkappa) proteins to activate target genes, such as the Hairy/Enhancer of Split (HES) family of bHLH transcriptional repressors (also known as bHLH-O). Activated bHLH-O inhibits the activity of AS-C genes in Notch signal-receiving cells (epidermal cells) and also prevents these cells from differentiating into SOPs [17], [18]. Failure to transmit the Delta-Notch lateral inhibition signal results in overproduction of SOPs in Drosophila[19]. In zebrafish embryos, the epidermal ICs are surrounded by keratinocytes which are arranged in a mosaic pattern. This observation suggests that epidermal IC cell fate choice might also be mediated by the Delta-Notch lateral inhibition mechanism. We tested this hypothesis by examining the epidermal IC development in several genetic mutants or morphants which have defects in the Delta-Notch signaling pathway. Results demonstrated that Delta-Notch-mediated lateral inhibition indeed plays a role in selecting epidermal IC progenitors from the epidermal stem cell (SC) pool in zebrafish.
###end p 10
###begin p 11
###xml 174 178 174 178 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0000302-Kaufmann1">[20]</xref>
###xml 180 184 180 184 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0000302-Carlsson1">[21]</xref>
###xml 186 190 186 190 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0000302-Lehmann1">[22]</xref>
###xml 192 196 192 196 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0000302-Lantz1">[23]</xref>
###xml 198 202 198 202 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0000302-Lam1">[24]</xref>
###xml 332 337 332 337 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">foxi1</italic>
###xml 421 426 421 426 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">foxi1</italic>
###xml 590 593 590 593 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0000302-Hulander1">[3]</xref>
###xml 627 631 627 631 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0000302-Blomqvist1">[25]</xref>
###xml 638 643 638 643 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">foxi1</italic>
###xml 915 919 915 919 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">foxi</italic>
###xml 1004 1009 1004 1009 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">foxi1</italic>
###xml 1011 1015 1011 1015 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0000302-Solomon1">[26]</xref>
###xml 1071 1076 1071 1076 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">foxi2</italic>
###xml 1078 1082 1078 1082 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0000302-Solomon2">[27]</xref>
###xml 1105 1111 1105 1111 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">foxi3a</italic>
###xml 1116 1122 1116 1122 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">foxi3b</italic>
###xml 1140 1146 1140 1146 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">foxi3a</italic>
###xml 1152 1158 1152 1158 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">foxi3b</italic>
###xml 1208 1212 1208 1212 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0000302-Solomon2">[27]</xref>
###xml 1259 1264 1259 1264 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">foxi3</italic>
###xml 1269 1275 1269 1275 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">foxi3b</italic>
###xml 1500 1506 1500 1506 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">foxi3a</italic>
###xml 1511 1517 1511 1517 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">foxi3b</italic>
###xml 888 897 <span type="species:ncbi:7955">zebrafish</span>
Winged helix/forkhead box transcription factors encode a large class of nuclear DNA-binding factors and function as cell fate determinants or cell differentiation regulators [20], [21], [22], [23], [24]. Recent studies have shown that IC differentiation in the mammalian inner ear and kidney is controlled by a strikingly conserved foxi1-dependent mechanism. Gene knockout experiments demonstrated that in the absence of foxi1, IC progenitors in the endolymphatic duct and collecting duct fail to differentiate into FORE cells and intercalated cells which respectively resulted in deafness [3] or distal renal tubular acidosis [25]. This foxi1-mediated process of IC differentiation in the inner ear and urinary system of terrestrial vertebrates inspired us to postulate whether a similar mechanism is also utilized by aquatic vertebrates for controlling epidermal IC differentiation. In zebrafish, four members of foxi genes have been identified as being expressed in the inner ear and pharyngeal arch (foxi1) [26], in the chordamesoderm, retina, and pharyngeal arches (foxi2) [27], and in mucous cells (foxi3a and foxi3b, we have renamed foxi3a- and foxi3b-expressing cells as epidermal ICs in this study) [27]. In this study, we manipulate the function of foxi3 and foxi3b by morpholino knockdown and mRNA misexpression to test whether they have a function in epidermal IC development. The data obtained from gain- and loss-of-function assays led us to discover a positive regulatory loop between foxi3a and foxi3b which is essential not only for acquiring IC identity but also for promoting IC differentiation.
###end p 11

###end title 12
###begin title 13
###xml 2 3 2 3 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>

###xml 62 63 62 63 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
Na+,K+-ATAPase-rich Cells (NaRCs) Differentiate Earlier than H+-ATPase-rich Cells (HRCs)
###end title 13
###begin p 14
###xml 175 176 175 176 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>

###xml 232 236 232 236 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0000302-Lin1">[14]</xref>
###xml 338 339 338 339 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 338 339 338 339 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><sup>+</sup></italic>
###xml 338 339 338 339 <bold xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><italic><sup>+</sup></italic></bold>
###xml 341 342 341 342 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 341 342 341 342 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><sup>+</sup></italic>
###xml 341 342 341 342 <bold xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><italic><sup>+</sup></italic></bold>
###xml 355 356 355 356 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 355 356 355 356 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><sup>+</sup></italic>
###xml 355 356 355 356 <bold xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><italic><sup>+</sup></italic></bold>
###xml 655 656 655 656 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 655 656 655 656 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><sup>+</sup></italic>
###xml 655 656 655 656 <bold xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><italic><sup>+</sup></italic></bold>
###xml 658 659 658 659 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>

###xml 1657 1663 1642 1648 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="pone-0000302-g001">ure 1A</xref>
###xml 1882 1886 1867 1871 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0000302-Lin1">[14]</xref>
###xml 2046 2055 2031 2040 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="pone-0000302-g001">Figure 1B</xref>
###xml 2137 2153 2122 2138 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="pone-0000302-g001">Figure 1A and 1B</xref>
###xml 2231 2238 2216 2223 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">atp1b1b</italic>
###xml 2243 2247 2228 2232 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ca2a</italic>
###xml 2249 2258 2234 2243 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="pone-0000302-g001">Figure 1B</xref>
###xml 2300 2307 2285 2292 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">atp1b1b</italic>
###xml 2420 2436 2405 2421 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="pone-0000302-g001">Figure 1C and 1D</xref>
###xml 2520 2527 2505 2512 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">atp1b1b</italic>
###xml 2539 2543 2524 2528 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ca2a</italic>
###xml 2599 2606 2584 2591 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">atp1b1b</italic>
###xml 2633 2637 2618 2622 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ca2a</italic>
###xml 126 135 <span type="species:ncbi:7955">zebrafish</span>
We have previously characterized two distinct populations of epidermal ICs, NaRCs and HRCs, in 3-day post-fertilization (dpf) zebrafish embryos which respectively expressed Na+,K+-ATPase and H+-ATPase in a mutually exclusive manner [14]. However, we failed to detect the early ontogeny of NaRCs and HRCs due to the low concentration of Na+,K+-ATPase and H+-ATPase which accumulated in epidermal ICs of embryos younger than 36 hours post-fertilization (hpf) (data not shown), indicating the difficulty in certifying the lineage relationships between NaRCs and HRCs by immunocytochemistry. To overcome this constraint, we tested several NaRC markers: the Na+,K+-ATPase alpha1a.2 subunit (atp1a1a.2), Na+,K+-ATPase alpha1a.4 subunit (atp1a1a.4), Na+,K+-ATPase alpha1a.5 subunit (atp1a1a.5), Na+,K+-ATPase beta1b subunit (atp1b1b), N-myc downstream-regulated gene 1 (ndrg1), and potassium inwardly-rectifying channel, subfamily J, member 1 (kcnj1), as well as HRC markers: the H+-ATPase V0c subunit (atp6v0c), type II arginase (arg2), transient receptor potential cation channel, subfamily M, member 7 (trpm7), and carbonic anhydrase 2a (ca2a) by in situ hybridization. Among these epidermal IC markers, atp1b1b and ca2a were identified as being robust and early markers for labeling NaRCs and HRCs, respectively (atp1b1b is also weakly expressed in the differentiating HRC, see below for discussion). By performing fluorescent double in situ hybridization, we found that atp1b1b was expressed on epidermal ICs with a sporadic pattern as early as 14 hpf (data not shown). By the 18-somite (18-s) stage, ca2a was detected in about 10% of atp1b1b-positive cells (ure 1A, highlighted by asterisks). By 24 hpf, the differentiating NaRCs and HRCs had gradually spread out and were scattered on the epidermal layer in a pattern similar to those detected by antibody staining in 3-dpf embryos [14]: differentiating HRCs were largely distributed on the yolk sac and extension regions, while differentiating NaRCs were more widely scattered on the epidermis (Figure 1B). Interestingly, neither NaRCs nor HRCs ever appeared on the cephalic epidermis (Figure 1A and 1B). For 24-hpf embryos, the differentiating HRCs expressed a balanced level of atp1b1b and ca2a (Figure 1B). However, as development proceeded, the atp1b1b level in differentiating HRCs was gradually downregulated and maintained at a basal expression level by 72 hpf (Figure 1C and 1D, labeled by asterisks). Differentiating NaRCs, on the contrary, strongly expressed atp1b1b but lacked ca2a expression. This observation suggests that both NaRCs (atp1b1b-positive cells) and HRCs (ca2a-positive cells) might come from common progenitors and then subsequently differentiate.
###end p 14

###xml 2118 2125 2118 2125 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">foxi3b-</italic>
###xml 2177 2183 2177 2183 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">foxi3a</italic>
###xml 2187 2193 2187 2193 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">foxi3b</italic>
###xml 2232 2233 2232 2233 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 2232 2233 2232 2233 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><sup>+</sup></italic>
###xml 2232 2233 2232 2233 <bold xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><italic><sup>+</sup></italic></bold>
###xml 2235 2236 2235 2236 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 2235 2236 2235 2236 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><sup>+</sup></italic>
###xml 2235 2236 2235 2236 <bold xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><italic><sup>+</sup></italic></bold>
###xml 2269 2270 2269 2270 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 2269 2270 2269 2270 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><sup>+</sup></italic>
###xml 2269 2270 2269 2270 <bold xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><italic><sup>+</sup></italic></bold>
###xml 2333 2339 2333 2339 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">foxi3a</italic>
###xml 2383 2392 2383 2392 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="pone-0000302-g001">Figure 1L</xref>
###xml 2412 2418 2412 2418 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">foxi3b</italic>
###xml 2449 2458 2449 2458 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="pone-0000302-g001">Figure 1M</xref>
###xml 2504 2513 2504 2513 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="pone-0000302-g001">Figure 1M</xref>
###xml 2564 2570 2564 2570 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">foxi3a</italic>
###xml 2575 2581 2575 2581 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">foxi3b</italic>
###xml 2809 2818 2809 2818 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="supplementary-material" rid="pone.0000302.s001">Figure S1</xref>
###xml 3140 3146 3140 3146 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">foxi3a</italic>
###xml 3151 3158 3151 3158 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">foxi3b)</italic>
###xml 3195 3202 3195 3202 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">atp1b1b</italic>
###xml 3207 3211 3207 3211 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ca2a</italic>
###xml 3334 3340 3334 3340 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">foxi3a</italic>
###xml 3345 3351 3345 3351 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">foxi3b</italic>
###xml 3367 3376 3367 3376 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="pone-0000302-g001">Figure 1N</xref>
###xml 2899 2908 <span type="species:ncbi:7955">zebrafish</span>
To test the common progenitor hypothesis, we needed a molecular marker to trace the early epidermal IC development before the onset of differentiation. foxi3a and foxi3b are duplicated winged helix/forkhead transcription factors and are sporadically expressed on the epidermis at the gastrula stage [27]. According to fluorescent double in situ hybridization, foxi3a was expressed within a subdomain (hereafter, defined as the epidermal IC domain) of the ventral ectoderm at around the 90% epiboly to tail bud (tb) stages (Figure 2A). By the 5-s stage, foxi3b was rapidly upregulated in the majority of foxi3a-positive cells and resulted in a mixed population of cells with either foxi3ahigh (red), foxi3ahigh/foxi3blow (yellow), or foxi3alow/foxi3bhigh (light green) expression (Figure 1E). By the 14-s stage, foxi3b expression in foxi3a-positive cells was sharply upregulated, resulting in more cells showing foxi3alow/foxi3bhigh (green) expression (Figure 1F). It is worth noting that by the 14-s stage, foxi3a expression in some foxi3alow/foxi3bhigh cells was sharply downregulated to a very low level, suggesting that these cells were differentiating into different cell fates. From the 18-s stage onwards, the differentiation process may have been completed since the majority of cells expressed either foxi3bhigh or foxi3ahigh/foxi3blow (Figure 1G and 1H). By 24 hpf, we noted that the spatial distribution patterns of foxi3a and foxi3b were highly similar to those of HRCs and NaRCs (compare Figure 1B with 1H or [14]). The comparable distribution pattern persisted until at least 72 hpf (Figure 1I and 1J). foxi3a-positive cells had a restricted distribution on the yolk and the yolk extension region (similar to the ca2a expression pattern); while foxi3b-positive cells were widely scattered on the epidermis (similar to the atp1b1b expression pattern). In addition, we also noted that the colocalized expressions of foxi3a and foxi3b (foxi3ahigh/foxi3blow) in the yolk extension region (Figure 1K) were highly similar to those of ca2a and atp1b1b (Figure 1C). To clarify the identity of foxi3a-positive and foxi3b-positive cells, we conducted triple-labeling with a foxi3a or foxi3b riboprobe together with NaRC- (anti-Na+,K+-ATPase) and HRC-specific (anti-H+-ATPase) antibodies on 72-hpf embryos. The results showed that foxi3a was indeed specifically expressed in HRCs (Figure 1L, red arrow), while foxi3b was weakly expressed in HRCs (Figure 1M, red arrow) but strongly expressed in NaRCs (Figure 1M, green arrow). This observation demonstrated that foxi3a and foxi3b are good markers not only for epidermal IC progenitors (before 14 s) but also for respectively differentiating HRCs and NaRCs. By cross-referencing the marker gene expression as well as the apical opening of epidermal ICs (see Figure S1 for details), we categorized three major development events of epidermal ICs in zebrafish as the progenitor stage (from the 90% epiboly to the 14-s stage), differentiation stage (from the 14-s stage to 36 hpf), and maturation stage (from 36 hpf onwards). According to the successive expression of the progenitor markers (foxi3a and foxi3b) as well as differentiation markers (atp1b1b and ca2a), we proposed that NaRCs and HRCs may be generated from common progenitors by the differentially regulated expressions of foxi3a and foxi3b (summarized in Figure 1N).
###end p 16
###begin title 17
Lateral Inhibition Plays a Role in Restricting the Number of Epidermal IC Progenitors
###end title 17
###begin p 18
###xml 22 28 22 28 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">foxi3a</italic>
###xml 154 160 154 160 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">foxi3a</italic>
###xml 193 197 193 197 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0000302-Bakkers1">[28]</xref>
###xml 216 222 216 222 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">foxi3a</italic>
###xml 297 306 297 306 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="pone-0000302-g002">Figure 2A</xref>
###xml 391 397 391 397 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">foxi3a</italic>
###xml 506 522 506 522 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="pone-0000302-g002">Figure 2B and 2C</xref>
###xml 805 814 805 814 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="pone-0000302-g002">Figure 2C</xref>
###xml 960 965 960 961 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">&#947;</italic>
###xml 1060 1064 1056 1060 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0000302-Geling1">[29]</xref>
###xml 1143 1149 1139 1145 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">foxi3a</italic>
###xml 1206 1227 1202 1223 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="sec" rid="s4">Materials and Methods</xref>
###xml 1279 1288 1275 1284 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="pone-0000302-g002">Figure 2D</xref>
###xml 1351 1357 1347 1353 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">foxi3a</italic>
###xml 1448 1457 1444 1453 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="pone-0000302-g002">Figure 2E</xref>
###xml 1478 1485 1474 1481 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="table" rid="pone-0000302-t001">Table 1</xref>
###xml 1674 1680 1670 1676 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">foxi3a</italic>
###xml 1743 1752 1739 1748 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">mind bomb</italic>
###xml 1765 1770 1761 1766 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ta52b</sup>
###xml 1762 1770 1758 1766 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">mib<sup>ta52b</sup></italic>
###xml 1939 1943 1935 1939 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0000302-Jiang1">[30]</xref>
###xml 1945 1949 1941 1945 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0000302-Itoh1">[31]</xref>
###xml 1990 1996 1986 1992 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">foxi3a</italic>
###xml 2014 2019 2010 2015 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ta52b</sup>
###xml 2011 2019 2007 2015 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">mib<sup>ta52b</sup></italic>
###xml 2029 2038 2025 2034 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="pone-0000302-g002">Figure 2G</xref>
###xml 2040 2047 2036 2043 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="table" rid="pone-0000302-t001">Table 1</xref>
###xml 2105 2110 2101 2106 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ta52b</sup>
###xml 2102 2110 2098 2106 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">mib<sup>ta52b</sup></italic>
###xml 2175 2181 2171 2177 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">deltaC</italic>
###xml 3 12 <span type="species:ncbi:7955">zebrafish</span>
###xml 2351 2360 <span type="species:ncbi:7955">zebrafish</span>
In zebrafish embryos, foxi3a-positive epidermal IC progenitors appear on the IC domain as early as the 90% epiboly stage. By examining the expressions of foxi3a and epidermal SC markers of P63 [28], we observed that foxi3a is co-expressed in a subpopulation of P63-positive cells at the tb stage (Figure 2A, labeled by asterisks). However, from the tb stage onwards, nuclear P63 staining in foxi3a-positive IC progenitors was gradually downregulated and eventually reached an undetectable level by 24 hpf (Figure 2B and 2C). This observation suggests that epidermal IC progenitors may be generated from the epidermal SC. We were interested in the molecular mechanism controlling the cell fate choice between epidermal ICs and epidermal SCs. Based on the mosaic pattern of epidermal ICs and epidermal SCs (Figure 2C), we speculated that a D-N-mediated lateral inhibition mechanism may be involved. To test this hypothesis, we initially treated embryos with the gamma-secretase inhibitor, DAPT, to inhibit Notch proteolysis, nuclear translocation, and signaling [29]. The DAPT-treated or control embryos were then fixed at the tb stage, and the foxi3a expression area was quantified by imaging software (see Materials and Methods). By comparing the sporadic pattern in wild-types (Figure 2D) or the DMSO sham control (data not shown), it was noted that foxi3a expression in DAPT-treated embryos had become about 2.2-fold denser and more homogeneous (Figure 2E; also summarized in Table 1). This result suggests that Notch signaling might play a role in regulating the IC progenitor number at the onset of IC specification. To confirm this preliminary observation, we examined foxi3a-positive epidermal IC progenitors in an antimorphic allele of mind bomb mutants (mibta52b), which fails in lateral inhibition due to widely compromised Notch signaling and results in premature differentiation of neural progenitors and neuron mass-production [30], [31]. We also detected about 2.3-fold denser foxi3a expression in mibta52b mutants (Figure 2G; Table 1) than siblings. In addition, in both DAPT-treated and mibta52b embryos, loss of Notch activity was confirmed by desynchronized deltaC expression in somite stripes (data not shown). Therefore, we confirmed that Notch signaling indeed plays a role in restricting the number of epidermal IC progenitors in zebrafish embryos.
###end p 18
###begin p 19
###xml 230 241 230 241 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">X-Su(H)/Ank</italic>
###xml 282 285 282 285 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">DBM</sup>
###xml 275 285 275 285 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">X-Su(H)<sup>DBM</sup></italic>
###xml 285 289 285 289 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0000302-Wettstein1">[32]</xref>
###xml 560 563 560 563 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">DBM</sup>
###xml 553 563 553 563 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">X-Su(H)<sup>DBM</sup></italic>
###xml 728 739 728 739 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">X-Su(H)/Ank</italic>
###xml 818 824 818 824 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">foxi3a</italic>
###xml 903 914 903 914 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">X-Su(H)/Ank</italic>
###xml 938 947 938 947 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="pone-0000302-g002">Figure 2I</xref>
###xml 949 956 949 956 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="table" rid="pone-0000302-t001">Table 1</xref>
###xml 965 971 965 971 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">foxi3a</italic>
###xml 993 996 993 996 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">DBM</sup>
###xml 986 996 986 996 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">X-Su(H)<sup>DBM</sup></italic>
###xml 1047 1056 1047 1056 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="pone-0000302-g002">Figure 2H</xref>
###xml 1058 1065 1058 1065 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="table" rid="pone-0000302-t001">Table 1</xref>
###xml 1155 1166 1155 1166 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">X-Su(H)/Ank</italic>
###xml 1178 1181 1178 1181 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">DBM</sup>
###xml 1171 1181 1171 1181 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">X-Su(H)<sup>DBM</sup></italic>
###xml 1275 1284 1275 1284 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="pone-0000302-g002">Figure 2E</xref>
###xml 1286 1293 1286 1293 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="table" rid="pone-0000302-t001">Table 1</xref>
###xml 1462 1467 1462 1467 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ta52b</sup>
###xml 1459 1467 1459 1467 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">mib<sup>ta52b</sup></italic>
###xml 1478 1481 1478 1481 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">DBM</sup>
###xml 1471 1481 1471 1481 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">X-Su(H)<sup>DBM</sup></italic>
###xml 1509 1515 1509 1515 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">foxi3a</italic>
###xml 1714 1720 1714 1720 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">foxi3a</italic>
Whether epidermal ICs behave as D-N signal-sending or -receiving cells was unknown. We attempted to determine this by manipulating the Suppressor of Hairless [Su(H)] activity with mRNAs encoding either the dominant-active form of X-Su(H)/Ank or the dominant-negative form of X-Su(H)DBM[32], The Su(H) is a component downstream of Notch signaling and functions as an effector to prevent receiving cell from adopting a default cell fate. If epidermal ICs behave as D-N signal-sending cells, it is expected that excessive epidermal ICs will be detected in X-Su(H)DBM mRNA-injected embryos (with reduced Notch activity). On the contrary, if epidermal SCs behave as D-N signal-sending cells, excessive epidermal ICs should appear in X-Su(H)/Ank mRNA-injected embryos (with enhanced Notch activity). Our results showed that foxi3a-positive epidermal ICs were sharply reduced in or even completely absent from X-Su(H)/Ank mRNA-injected embryos (Figure 2I; Table 1), while foxi3a expression in X-Su(H)DBM mRNA-injected embryos became about 2-fold denser (Figure 2H; Table 1). The reduced or excessive epidermal IC phenotype is unlikely to be due to toxicity when X-Su(H)/Ank- or X-Su(H)DBM-mRNA is misexpressed, since no detrimental effect was observed in GFP-misexpressing embryos (Figure 2E; Table 1). Therefore, we concluded that epidermal ICs behave as D-N signal-sending cells while epidermal SCs serve as signal-receiving cells. Interestingly, in DAPT-treated, mibta52b or X-Su(H)DBM mRNA-injected embryos, the foxi3a-positive epidermal IC progenitors only appeared within the epidermal IC domain, but never extended outside the domain. This result suggests that there should be other mechanisms which delineate the foxi3a-positive epidermal IC domain first, and the D-N-mediated lateral inhibition subsequently functions within this domain to single out epidermal IC progenitors from epidermal SCs.
###end p 19
###begin title 20
###xml 43 48 43 48 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ta52b</sup>
###xml 40 48 40 48 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">mib<sup>ta52b</sup></italic>
The Excessive Epidermal IC Phenotype in mibta52b Mutants is at the Expanse of Epidermal SCs
###end title 20
###begin p 21
###xml 59 64 59 64 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ta52b</sup>
###xml 56 64 56 64 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">mib<sup>ta52b</sup></italic>
###xml 90 93 90 93 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">DBM</sup>
###xml 83 93 83 93 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">X-Su(H)<sup>DBM</sup></italic>
###xml 219 225 219 225 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">foxi3a</italic>
###xml 348 354 348 354 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">foxi3a</italic>
###xml 384 389 384 389 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ta52b</sup>
###xml 381 389 381 389 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">mib<sup>ta52b</sup></italic>
###xml 488 489 484 485 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">n</italic>
###xml 495 496 491 492 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">p</italic>
###xml 577 578 569 570 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">n</italic>
###xml 633 634 621 622 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">n</italic>
###xml 684 689 672 677 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ta52b</sup>
###xml 681 689 669 677 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">mib<sup>ta52b</sup></italic>
###xml 711 723 699 711 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="pone-0000302-g003">Figure 3A&#8211;3C</xref>
###xml 809 814 797 802 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ta52b</sup>
###xml 806 814 794 802 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">mib<sup>ta52b</sup></italic>
###xml 1117 1118 1101 1102 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">n</italic>
###xml 1124 1125 1108 1109 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">p</italic>
###xml 1157 1158 1137 1138 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">n</italic>
###xml 1164 1165 1144 1145 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">p</italic>
###xml 1219 1220 1195 1196 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">n</italic>
###xml 1226 1227 1202 1203 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">p</italic>
###xml 1240 1245 1216 1221 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ta52b</sup>
###xml 1237 1245 1213 1221 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">mib<sup>ta52b</sup></italic>
###xml 1383 1388 1359 1364 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ta52b</sup>
###xml 1380 1388 1356 1364 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">mib<sup>ta52b</sup></italic>
###xml 1410 1422 1386 1398 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="pone-0000302-g003">Figure 3D&#8211;3J</xref>
The excessive epidermal IC phenotype observed in either mibta52b, DAPT-treated, or X-Su(H)DBM mRNA-injected embryos might be generated by excessive cell division or by the cell fate choice. We examined cell division of foxi3a-stained embryos using phospho-histone 3 (pH3) antibody staining. Within the epidermal IC domain, we counted the number of foxi3a-positive epidermal ICs in mibta52b mutants and found that it was about 2-fold higher than that of its siblings (151+/-24 vs. 75+/-5, n = 7, p<0.05). However, the proliferation activity in whole embryos (28+/-4 vs. 33+/-8, n = 7) or in epidermal IC progenitors (7+/-0 vs. 5+/-2, n = 7) showed no significant difference between mibta52b and sibling embryos (Figure 3A-3C). Therefore, it is unlikely that the excessive epidermal IC phenotype observed in mibta52b mutants is generated by an excess of cell proliferation. Is it due to an aberrant cell fate choice? By counting the number of epidermal ICs (including both NaRCs and HRCs) and epidermal SCs (by P63 antibody staining) at 24 hpf, we detected significantly higher epidermal ICs (NaRCs, 41+/-5 vs. 28+/-5, n = 7, p<0.05; HRCs, 88+/-8 vs. 21+/-3, n = 7, p<0.05) and lower epidermal SCs (135+/-8 vs. 212+/-11, n = 7, p<0.05) in mibta52b mutants. When the total number of epidermal ICs and epidermal SCs was taken into account, there was no significant difference between mibta52b and sibling embryos (Figure 3D-3J). These results clearly demonstrate that excessive epidermal ICs occur at the expense of epidermal SC cell fate, and also suggest the default cell fate of the IC domain is set as epidermal ICs when the Notch signal is absent. However, when Notch activity is enhanced, the entire epidermal IC domain will adopt an epidermal SC cell fate. Therefore, we concluded that a balanced mixture of epidermal ICs and epidermal SCs within the IC domain is maintained by the D-N-mediated lateral inhibition mechanism.
###end p 21

###xml 1474 1483 1474 1483 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="pone-0000302-g004">Figure 4A</xref>
###xml 1570 1579 1570 1579 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="pone-0000302-g004">Figure 4A</xref>
###xml 1584 1590 1584 1590 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">deltaC</italic>
###xml 1629 1630 1615 1616 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">n</italic>
###xml 1662 1668 1648 1654 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">foxi3a</italic>
###xml 1707 1708 1679 1680 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">n</italic>
###xml 1762 1768 1734 1740 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">deltaC</italic>
###xml 1818 1825 1790 1797 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">foxi3a-</italic>
###xml 1835 1844 1807 1816 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="pone-0000302-g004">Figure 4B</xref>
###xml 1872 1878 1844 1850 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">deltaC</italic>
###xml 1888 1894 1860 1866 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">foxi3a</italic>
###xml 1990 1996 1962 1968 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">deltaC</italic>
###xml 2081 2087 2053 2059 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">foxi3a</italic>
###xml 2089 2105 2061 2077 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="pone-0000302-g004">Figure 4C and 4D</xref>
###xml 2124 2130 2096 2102 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">foxi3a</italic>
###xml 2161 2167 2133 2139 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">deltaC</italic>
###xml 2246 2255 2218 2227 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="pone-0000302-g004">Figure 4D</xref>
###xml 245 254 <span type="species:ncbi:7955">zebrafish</span>
###xml 1038 1047 <span type="species:ncbi:7955">zebrafish</span>
###xml 1335 1344 <span type="species:ncbi:7955">zebrafish</span>
D-N-mediated lateral inhibition plays essential roles in the cell fate choice and cell differentiation of diverse target tissues by utilizing different combinations of Delta/Jagged ligands and Notch receptors in a stepwise manner [33], [34]. In zebrafish, at least eight delta/jagged genes: deltaA[35], deltaB[36], deltaC[37], deltaD[38], delta-like 4 (dll4)[39], jagged1a[40], jagged1b[40], and jaggged2[36], [40], and four notch genes: notch1a[41], notch1b[42], notch2[42], and notch3, [42] have been identified so far. To figure out which combination of ligand and receptor is used, we performed in situ hybridization with deltaA, deltaB, deltaC, deltaD, dll4, jagged1a, jagged1b, and jaggged2 probes to look for possible delta/jagged components expressed on epidermal IC progenitors around the tb to 3-s stages. As a result, only deltaC was detected as being expressed on the ventral ectoderm in a salt-and-pepper pattern (Figure S2). This result suggests that deltaC may be the only ligand involved in mediating IC specification. In zebrafish, although the epidermal expression of deltaC has previously been described [37], the identity of deltaC-positive cells is unknown. To validate whether deltaC is expressed in the epidermal IC lineage, we performed fluorescent double in situ hybridization with deltaC and foxi3a probes on zebrafish embryos at the tb stage. Results demonstrated that both foxi3a and deltaC were largely co-expressed in epidermal IC progenitors (Figure 4A). However, we noted that the territory (expressed as the degree of the angle shown in Figure 4A) of deltaC-positive cells (69degrees+/-4degrees, n = 16) was greater than that of foxi3a-positive cells (49degrees+/-2degrees, n = 11). A high-magnification view confirmed that most deltaC-positive cells outside the IC domain were indeed foxi3a-negative (Figure 4B). We speculated that these deltaC-positive/foxi3a-negative cells might be the progenitors for other epidermal cell types. It was also noted that deltaC expression on the epidermal IC lineage was relatively transient compared to that of foxi3a (Figure 4C and 4D). By 24 hpf, when foxi3a was robustly being expressed, deltaC was sharply downregulated to an undetectable level in the epidermal ectoderm (Figure 4D).
###end p 23

###xml 609 615 609 615 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">notch3</italic>
###xml 673 679 673 679 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">foxi3a</italic>
###xml 731 737 731 737 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">deltaC</italic>
###xml 913 919 913 919 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">foxi3a</italic>
###xml 923 929 923 929 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">deltaC</italic>
###xml 933 939 933 939 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">tit446</sup>
###xml 930 939 930 939 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">bea<sup>tit446</sup></italic>
###xml 941 950 941 950 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="pone-0000302-g004">Figure 4E</xref>
###xml 952 959 952 959 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="table" rid="pone-0000302-t001">Table 1</xref>

###xml 1873 1880 1873 1880 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="table" rid="pone-0000302-t001">Table 1</xref>
###xml 1961 1968 1961 1968 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">notch1a</italic>
###xml 1973 1979 1973 1979 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">notch3</italic>
###xml 2037 2043 2037 2043 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">foxi3a</italic>
###xml 2189 2196 2189 2196 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">notch1a</italic>
###xml 2198 2207 2198 2207 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="pone-0000302-g004">Figure 4J</xref>
###xml 2209 2216 2209 2216 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="table" rid="pone-0000302-t001">Table 1</xref>
###xml 2221 2227 2221 2227 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">notch3</italic>
###xml 2229 2238 2229 2238 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="pone-0000302-g004">Figure 4K</xref>
###xml 2240 2247 2240 2247 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="table" rid="pone-0000302-t001">Table 1</xref>
###xml 2284 2291 2284 2291 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">notch1a</italic>
###xml 2327 2333 2327 2333 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">notch3</italic>
###xml 2347 2353 2347 2353 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">deltaC</italic>
###xml 2419 2437 2419 2437 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">notch1a and notch3</italic>
Next, we searched for possible Notch receptors by performing in situ hybridization with notch1a, notch1b, notch2, and notch3 probes. Results showed that notch1a was diffusely and strongly expressed in the ventral ectoderm (Figure S3) and possibly functions as a receptor to receive DeltaC signals. Other notch genes, on the contrary, were only expressed in the ventral ectoderm at a basal level (Figure S3). To further validate the right components for transmitting lateral inhibition for selecting epidermal IC progenitors, we assayed the loss-of-function phenotypes of deltaC, notch1a, notch1b, notch2, and notch3 genes in genetic mutants or morphants by examining their foxi3a expression at the tb stage. Results confirmed that deltaC is indeed a crucial ligand and the only one which sends a lateral inhibition signal to single out epidermal IC progenitors, since the entire IC domain homogeneously expressed foxi3a in deltaC/beatit446 (Figure 4E; Table 1) and deltaC/beatw212b mutants (Figure 4F; Table 1). In notch1a/desth35b mutants, we also detected an increase in foxi3a expression within the IC domain (Figure 4G; Table 1). However, its foxi3a-positive area was much less dense than those of mibta52b, beatit446, or beatw212b mutants (Table 1). These results suggest that there should be other notch receptor(s) which synergistically function with notch1a in mediating lateral inhibition. We explored this possibility by injecting notch1b morpholino (MO), notch2 MO, or notch3 MO into desth35b mutants and assaying foxi3a expression at the tb stage. As a result, we failed to detect any significant increase in the foxi3a-positive area when notch1b MO (Figure 4H; Table 1) or notch2 MO (data not shown) was injected into desth35b mutants. However, when notch3 MO was injected into desth35b mutants, the entire IC domain homogeneously expressed foxi3a (Figure 4I; Table 1). In addition to the loss-of-function assay, we also validated the functions of notch1a and notch3 through a gain-of-function approach. As we expected, the foxi3a-positive area was greatly reduced or even completely lost when we enhanced Notch activity by misexpressing intracellular domains (ICD) of either notch1a (Figure 4J; Table 1) or notch3 (Figure 4K; Table 1). Taken together, we concluded that notch1a plays a more-predominant role than notch3 in receiving deltaC signals from epidermal ICs. In addition, the cooperation between notch1a and notch3 carries out D-N-mediated lateral inhibition in a synergistic manner to maintain a balanced population of epidermal ICs and SCs.
###end p 24
###begin title 25
###xml 0 6 0 6 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">deltaC</italic>
###xml 88 94 88 94 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ascl1a</italic>
###xml 99 104 99 104 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">notch</italic>
deltaC Expression in Epidermal IC Progenitors is Positively and Negatively Regulated by ascl1a and notch, Respectively
###end title 25
###begin p 26
###xml 3 13 3 13 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">Drosophila</italic>
###xml 19 24 19 24 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">delta</italic>
###xml 148 152 148 152 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0000302-Heitzler1">[18]</xref>
###xml 154 158 154 158 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0000302-Heitzler2">[19]</xref>
###xml 464 470 464 470 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">deltaC</italic>
###xml 682 688 682 688 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ascl1a</italic>
###xml 690 719 690 719 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">achaete-scute complex-like 1a</italic>
###xml 827 833 827 833 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">deltaC</italic>
###xml 882 888 882 888 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">deltaC</italic>
###xml 908 914 908 914 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ascl1a</italic>
###xml 1015 1021 1015 1021 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ascl1a</italic>
###xml 1104 1110 1104 1110 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ascl1a</italic>
###xml 1128 1139 1128 1139 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">VP16:ascl1a</italic>
###xml 1159 1163 1159 1163 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pone.0000302-Herzog1">[43]</xref>
###xml 1206 1217 1206 1217 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">VP16:aslc1a</italic>
###xml 1307 1313 1307 1313 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">deltaC</italic>
###xml 1383 1392 1383 1392 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="pone-0000302-g005">Figure 5A</xref>
###xml 1456 1462 1456 1462 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">foxi3a</italic>
###xml 1496 1505 1496 1505 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="pone-0000302-g005">Figure 5A</xref>
###xml 1556 1562 1556 1562 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ascl1a</italic>
###xml 1576 1582 1576 1582 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">deltaC</italic>
###xml 1591 1597 1591 1597 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">foxi3a</italic>
###xml 1630 1636 1630 1636 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">deltaC</italic>
###xml 411 420 <span type="species:ncbi:7955">zebrafish</span>
###xml 1250 1259 <span type="species:ncbi:7955">zebrafish</span>
In Drosophila, the delta expression in signal-sending cells is positively enhanced by a proneuronal gene but negatively regulated by Notch activity [18], [19]. This feedback regulatory loop can amplify the tiny difference in Delta expression between cells in a tissue at the beginning of the cell fate choice stage, and gradually specifies the cell identity between pure signal-sending and -receiving cells. In zebrafish, we were curious about whether the initial deltaC expression in epidermal IC progenitors is also positively activated by a proneural gene and negatively regulated by the Notch negative regulatory loop. To test this hypothesis, we first forced expression of the ascl1a (achaete-scute complex-like 1a) proneural gene (also expressed in epidermal IC progenitors, data not shown) by mRNA injection and assayed deltaC expression at the tb stage. Results showed that deltaC was upregulated by ascl1a mRNA misexpression; however, the penetrance was low (data not shown). To exaggerate the function of ascl1a, we fused a strong activation domain from a herpes simplex virus with full-length ascl1a cDNA to generate VP16:ascl1a chimera constructs [43]. Interestingly, when enhanced versions of VP16:aslc1a mRNA were forcedly expressed in zebrafish embryos, this was sufficient to induce ectopic deltaC expression in the cephalic epidermal ectoderm outside the IC domain (Figure 5A, green panel, indicated by arrows), but insufficient to induce foxi3a expression in this ectopic site (Figure 5A, red panel). These results indicated that, first, ascl1a can regulate deltaC but not foxi3a expression; and second, ectopic deltaC-expressing cells outside the IC domain are not epidermal ICs.
###end p 26
###begin p 27
###xml 50 56 50 56 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">deltaC</italic>
